S2.        RELATION TO DYNAMICAL ACTIVITY


We show that the term Φ in Eq. (9) serves a similar function to the dynamical activity in classical Markov processes, which plays a central role in trade-off relations [1–3].
We provide definitions of entropy production and dynamical activity in Markov processes. Let Wnm be the transition rate from the mth state to the nth state in a classical Markov process and let Pm(t) be the probability that the system is in mth state at time t. Given a Markov process P˙ (t) = WP (t), where W ≡ {Wnm}nm and P (t) = {Pm(t)}m is the
probability vector, the dynamical activity rate A˜˙ and the entropy production rate Σ˜˙ are defined by
Σ˜˙ ≡        (Pm  
n<m  
________________


Wnm
________________


− Pn
________________


Wmn
________________
) ln PmWnm ,        (S10)
PnWmn
A˜˙ ≡        (PmWnm + PnWmn) .        (S11)
n<m


For simplicity, we assume that the system is in the steady state. Let N (τ ) be an observable of a stochastic trajectory in the time interval [0, τ ], which is N (τ ) = 0 when there is no jump in the trajectory. In Ref. [4], it was shown that the probability of N (τ ) = 0 and the dynamical activity are related via
P (N (τ ) = 0) ≥ e−A˜.        (S12)
Analogously, in this Letter, we have derived the lower bound on P (G = 0) as follows:
P (G = 0)        dS e−Φ(β,∆ϵ,λmin(ρS )).        (S13)  
dE


From Eqs. (S12) and (S13), the key point of the analogy is that both e−A˜ and (dS/dE)e−Φ serve as quantities that determine lower bounds for the zero probabilities of observables.
Moreover, there is another indication which demonstrates the analogy between the dynamical activity and Φ in Eq. (9). It has been revealed that the dynamical activity rate constitutes an upper bound on the entropy production rate [5]:
Σ˜˙ ≤ κ(R)A˜˙,        (S14)
where R is the maximum transition rate ratio R  maxn̸=m Wmn/Wnm and κ(R)  (ln R)(R  1)/(R + 1). The entropy production can become arbitrarily large if we allow the jump rate ratio to be infinitely large. Equation (S14) states that, when there is the upper bound on the ratio Wmn/Wnm, as specified by R, the entropy production rate  
Σ˜˙ is bounded from above by the dynamical activity A˜. This is reminiscent of Eq. (14), showing that Φ serves as an
upper bound on the entropy production. These analogies suggest that Φ, as defined in Eq. (9), plays a role similar to that of the dynamical activity.


S3.        DERIVATION OF QUANTUM THERMODYNAMIC UNCERTAINTY RELATION


We present the derivation of a quantum thermodynamic uncertainty relation given by Eq. (15) in the main text, whose lower bound comprises the entropy production Σ. For arbitrary density operators ρ and σ, let Fid(ρ, σ) be the quantum fidelity:
2    
  
  

Fid(ρ, σ) ≡        Tr        ρσ  ρ        ,        (S15)


which quantifies the similarity between the two quantum states. Let D(ρ∥σ) be the quantum relative entropy:
D(ρ∥σ) ≡ Tr [ρ(ln ρ − ln σ)] .        (S16)
According to Theorem 7 in Ref. [6], the following relation holds between the fidelity Fid(ρ, σ) and the quantum relative entropy D(ρ∥σ):
e−D(ρ∥σ) ≤ Fid(ρ, σ).        (S17)
We next derive an upper bound on the quantum fidelity using the probabilities associated with measurements on the environment E. Let {Mx}x be a positive operator-valued measure (POVM) acting on the environment, and let PE(x) and P ′ (x) denote the probabilities of measuring x with the POVM, PE(x) = Tr[MxγE] and P ′ (x) = Tr[Mxρ′ ].      
Here, we assume that the measurement output x of        x is a real value. Moreover, the fidelity satisfies the following relation:  
qFid(ρ′ , γE) ≤ L qP ′ (x)PE(x)

S4.        RELATION TO OTHER UNCERTAINTY RELATIONS


   1. Joint unitary transformation formalism


In the main text, we derive the fundamental precision limits for the system described by the joint unitary evolution applied to the system and environment. Here, we review quantum thermodynamic uncertainty relations and the concept of entropy production in the joint unitary model.
In the joint unitary model, we prepare a state consisting of the system S and the environment E. Given the initial state ρSE = ρS ⊗ γE, where γE is the Gibbs state, we apply a unitary U to obtain
′        = U (ρS ⊗ γE)U †.        (S24)    
In Refs. [10, 11], the entropy production is studied for the joint unitary model. We can identify the heat flowing from the reservoir as
∆Q = Tr[HEγE] − Tr[HEρ′ ],        (S25)  
where HE is the Hamiltonian of the environment and ρ′ = TrS[ρ′ ] is the density operator after the unitary. The    
entropy flow is defined as
________________


∆Se = β∆Q,        (S26)
where β is the inverse temperature of the environment E. The change in entropy of the system is
∆S = −Tr[ρ′ ln ρ′ ] + Tr[ρS ln ρS].        (S27)    
According to the standard thermodynamic description, the entropy ∆ , entropy flow ∆ e, and entropy production Σ satisfy  
Σ = ∆S − ∆Se.        (S28)
Reference [10, 11] showed that Σ is expressed as the quantum relative entropy [Eq. (S16)]:
Σ = D(ρ′  ∥ρS ⊗ γE)    
= I(S′, E′) + D(ρ′ ∥γE) ≥ 0,        (S29)  
where I(·, ·) is the quantum mutual information:
′        ′        ′        ′        ′
I(S , E ) ≡ S(ρS ) + S(ρE ) − S(ρSE ).        (S30)
It is known that the quantum mutual information is always non-negative,  (S′, E′)  0.  
Reference [12] derived a quantum thermodynamic uncertainty relation for the joint unitary model. Let f (x) = 1/ sinh2[g(x)/2], where g(x) is the inverse of h(x) x tanh(x/2). For an arbitrary measurement operator G in the environment, from Ref. [12], it can be stated that  
 Varρ′ [G] + VarγE [G]        _Σ + Σ∗ )    
1 Eρ′ [G] − EγE [G]        
________________
)2 ≥ f
________________
,        (S31)
2
where Eρ [G] = Tr [ρG] and Varρ [G] = Tr (ρG2] − Tr [ρG]2 as defined in the main text. In Eq. (S31), Σ∗ is defined by
Σ∗ ≡ D(ρ′ ⊗ γE∥ρSE ).        (S32)    
In Eq. (S31), Σ∗ does not correspond to the standard entropy production. Although Eq. (S31) holds for a general joint unitary dynamics, the downside is that it depends on Σ∗, which has less physical interpretation. A similar relation can be derived using the properties of the quantum relative entropy. We can show that the following relation holds (see Section S3):
2          
′        VarγE [G]        1  
        Eρ′ [G] − EγE [G]          ≥ eΣ − 1 .        (S33)      
Equation (S33) is similar to Eq. (S31); however, it should be emphasized that the right-hand side only includes Σ. What these equations have in common is that they do not simply use the ratio of variance to mean, but rather employ two statistical quantities, one before and after the unitary evolution. When the dynamics are trivial (i.e., U = I), the entropy production vanishes (Σ = 0). In this case, both sides of Eq. (S33) diverge to infinity. This differs from Eq. (10) in the main text, which remains valid even when there is no dynamics.
So far, we are concerned with uncertainty relations whose lower bounds comprise the quantum entropy production. Let us comment on the difference between an uncertainty relation derived in Ref. [13], which also considered the joint unitary dynamics. Reference [13] considered the system S and environment E, where the initial environmental state is pure. Suppose that the initial environmental state is 0 without loss of generality. Then the time evolution in the system S is represented by a Kraus representation:  


′        †    
S        g
g
For a general observable G whose smallest eigenvalue is 0, Ref. [13] showed the following relation:
Varρ′ [G]        1  
E ′ [G]2 ≥ Tr 1(V †V0)−1ρS i ,        (S35)          
where V0 is associated with the measurement of G = 0. Using the Neumann series expansion, as demonstrated in
Ref. [14], we can express (V †V )−1 = L∞  (I − V †V )n. Under the first-order approximation, this result shows          
that the bound derived in Ref. [13] is equivalent to the Petrov inequality with a specific parameter value. Although the quantum thermodynamic uncertainty relation in Ref. [13] depends on the system dynamics, as evidenced by the dynamics-dependent Kraus operator V0 in Eq. (S35), the precision limit we derive in Eq. (10) is dynamics-independent.


   2. Continuous measurement formalism


The continuous measurement formalism is frequently employed in studies of thermodynamic uncertainty relations. An overview of this approach is provided below.
First, we consider the single reservoir case. Let β denote the inverse temperature of the reservoir. The Lindblad equation describes the time evolution of an open quantum system:
ρ˙(t) = L (ρ(t)) = −i [H, ρ(t)] + K (ρ(t)) ,        (S36)
where ρ(t) is the density operator at time t and L(ρ) is the Lindblad superoperator and K(ρ) is the dissipator:
K(ρ) ≡ L  L ρL†        1 L† L , ρ} .        (S37)          
  

Here, H denotes the Hamiltonian and Lm denotes the mth jump operator. For simplicity, we assume two jump operators in this example. The approach easily generalizes to cases with more than two jump operators. L1 and L2 are the pair of operators such that
[H, L1] = −ωL1,        [H, L2] = ωL2,        (S38)
where ω is the energy difference. Moreover, L1 and L2 satisfy the following detailed balance relation:
L† = eβω/2L2.        (S39)  
Equations (S38) and (S39) guarantee that the steady state of Eq. (S36) is the Gibbs state:
L(γ) = K(γ) = 0,        (S40)


where γ is the Gibbs state:

We now consider entropy production in the Lindblad equation. From the first law of thermodynamics, heat and work can be identified as
dE [H] =  d Tr [Hρ(t)] = Tr 1H˙ ρ(t)i + Tr [Hρ˙(t)] = W˜˙ + Q˜˙ ,        (S42)    
where W˜˙  and Q˜˙ denote the exerted work and the heat flowing into the system, respectively, in the Markov process
(the tilde is used to indicate that these quantities are associated with the Markov process). Here, the heat is expressed as
Q˜˙ = Tr [Hρ˙(t)] = Tr [HL (ρ(t))] = Tr [HK (ρ(t))] .        (S43)
Calculating the time derivative of the entropy        (ρ), from the standard thermodynamic description, we can identify the entropy production as  
Σ˜˙ = S˙ − S˜˙e,        (S44)

In the joint unitary description, the heat is identified as the energy difference in the environment E [Eq. (S25)]. On the other hand, in the Lindblad formalism, the heat can be calculated from quantities in the system alone. Note that the classical limit of Σ˜ is shown in Eq. (S10).
Next, we consider multiple reservoir cases, where the inverse temperature of ℓth reservoir is given by βℓ. Suppose that there are NR reservoirs. When there are multiple reservoirs, we can decompose the dynamics into those arising from each reservoir [15, 16]:

Under this assumption, the local Gibbs state becomes the steady-state solution of each dissipator:  
Kℓ(γℓ) = 0,        (S47)

The dynamical activity is another thermodynamic quantity, which is often employed in uncertainty relations. The dynamical activity in the Lindblad equation is given by
A˜˙ =

which quantifies the average number of jumps. Note that the classical definition of A˜ is shown in Eq. (S11).  
Let Nℓ,1 denote the number of jump events induced by Lℓ,1 in the ℓth reservoir during the time interval [0, τ ]. Similarly, Nℓ,2 is defined as the number of jump events induced by Lℓ,2 in the same reservoir and time interval. In the continuous measurement formalism, the observable is


NR  
C =        (cℓ,1Nℓ,1 + cℓ,2Nℓ,2) .        (S51)
ℓ=1
Here, cℓ,1 and cℓ,2 are weights for the jumps. When cℓ,1 and cℓ,2 satisfy the antisymmetry condition, cℓ,1 = cℓ,2, the observable corresponds to a thermodynamic current. For the classical limit of the Lindblad equation, the following relations are known to hold [1, 3, 17, 18]:  
Var[C]        2        Var[C]        1
E[C]2 ≥ Σ˜ ,        E[C]2 ≥ A˜.        (S52)        
Strictly speaking, the relation of the entropy production requires the antisymmetry condition, while the relation of the dynamical activity does not impose any constraint on the weights as long as cℓ,1 and cℓ,2 are real. In the presence of coherent dynamics, the expressions shown in Eq. (S52) do not hold. In quantum thermodynamic uncertainty relations based on dynamical activity, such as those discussed in Refs. [19, 20], quantum coherence is included as part of the definition of dynamical activity. A recent study [21] derived a thermodynamic uncertainty relation for dynamical activity in which the expectation value includes an additional term arising from coherent dynamics, rather than modifying the dynamical activity itself. Similarly, for quantum thermodynamic uncertainty relations based on entropy production, Refs. [22, 23] have shown that quantum coherence can enhance precision. This is achieved by introducing correction terms from coherent dynamics into the expectation value and the entropy production.
S5.        EQUALITY CONDITION


   1. Equality condition of Eq. (8)


We show the equality condition of the fundamental precision limit given by Eq. (8). The equality in Eq. (8) requires that every inequality used in its derivation saturates. The main result is built from five inequalities; therefore, Eq. (8) can be saturated only if all five hold with equality. We analyze the equality condition in reverse order.
First, Eq. (7):


e−βϵmax
λmin(γE) = Tr[e−βHE ] ≥ d

dS  
λ↑(ρSE) ≥ dSλmin(ρSE) = dSλmin(ρS)λmin(γE).        (S54)  
i=1
The equality holds when the dS smallest eigenvalues of ρSE are all equal to λmin(ρSE). When β = 0 (so γE is uniform), this is ensured whenever dE ζ dS, where ζ is the degeneracy of λmin(ρS). Therefore, the condition is automatically satisfied for dS  dE.    
For Eq. (B3):

Equality can be achieved by choosing U to be a permutation (swap) unitary that rearranges the eigenvalues so that, after tracing out S, the sum of the dS smallest eigenvalues of ρSE appears as the minimal diagonal entry of ρ′ .

   * β = 0 (γE is maximally mixed)
   * U is chosen as a permutation/swap that saturates Eq. (S55)
   * The eigenvector corresponding to the minimum eigenvalue 0 of the observable G agrees with the eigenvector corresponding to the minimum eigenvalue of ρ′  
   * G takes only two values
   * dE ζ ≥ dS for the minimum-eigenvalue degeneracy ζ of ρS


      2. Equality condition of Petrov inequality


Let us consider the equality condition of the Petrov inequality [24] for b = 0. Suppose that P (X) is a two-point distribution:
P (X = x1) = p1, P (X = x2) = p2 = 1 − p1,        (S58)

Next, we show that P (X) being a two-point distribution is the necessary and sufficient condition for the equality. Let us define a new random variable Y = (X X > 0), which is defined under the condition X > 0. For example, when the realization of X is [1, 2, 0, 3, 0], its corresponding realization of Y is [1, 2, 3]. The following relation holds:  


E [Xr] = E [Y r] P (X > 0).        (S62)

By Jensen’s inequality, the equality in Eq. (S64) holds when Y is constant, Y = c > 0. In terms of the original variable X, this corresponds to X taking only the values 0 and c, that is, a two-point distribution.




S6.        EXAMPLES


As applications of the fundamental precision limit, we consider two practical scenarios: the quantum battery model (Fig. S1(a)) and the collision model (Fig. S1(b)).
      1. Quantum battery


A quantum battery is an experimental energy storage device that uses quantum effects to store and release energy [25, 26]. They have the potential to charge faster, deliver higher power, and operate more efficiently than their classical
counterparts. In the pioneering work [27], the quantum battery is modeled as a finite-level system LdE ϵ |ϵ ⟩ ⟨ϵ |,        
which is identical to the environmental Hamiltonian defined in Eq. (2) in the main text. To charge or release energy in the quantum battery, several approaches exist. The simplest approach considers a unitary Uch, which is applied directly to the battery. Another option is the charger-mediated protocol, in which the charger is introduced to charge the battery. The charger initially contains energy which is transferred to the battery via the interaction (Fig. S1(a)). When we identify the charger and the battery as the system S and the environment E, respectively, the charger-mediated protocol falls into the open quantum model considered in the main text (Fig. 2(a) in the main text).
In the quantum battery, the primary quantity of interest is the amount of energy charged in the battery. When the Hamiltonian of the battery is HE, the stored energy is given by
EB ≡ Eρ′ [HE] − EρE [HE],        (S65)  
where ρ′  and ρE are the final and initial battery states, respectively. A primary objective is to design a quantum  
battery that maximizes B, since such a quantum battery can store more energy. In recent years, the charging precision has been emphasized as an alternative metric for quantum batteries [28, 29]. Studies [28, 29] used the post-charging energy variance as a measure of precision and sought a quantum battery that makes this variance as small as possible. This alternative metric, the charging precision, is given by  
PB ≡ Varρ′ [HE].        (S66)  
The performance of quantum batteries can be evaluated by EB and PB; a quantum battery with larger EB and smaller
2        2
PB is preferable. It is natural to consider the quantity PB/EB; a quantum battery with smaller PB/EB achieves the
maximization of EB and the minimization of PB. Without loss of generality, we set λmin(HE) = 0. Using Eq. (10) in
the main text, PB/E 2 has the following lower bound:  
PB        Varρ′ [HE ]        _ dE  Φ(β,∆ϵ,λmin(ρS ))        )−1    
2        Eρ′ [HE        
________________
]2 ≥        d e
________________
− 1        ,        (S67)
which is Eq. (12) in the main text. Equation (S67) shows that the stored energy B and the charging precision        B  
are in a trade-off relation.


      2. Collision model


The collision model is a framework to study open quantum dynamics [30]. In the collision model, the environment is represented as a sequence of ancillae, and the system interacts sequentially with each ancilla for a short time. The collision model is widely used to study the thermodynamic aspects of quantum systems [31–34]. Moreover, the collision model forms the basis for the continuous measurement formalism [35], which is often considered in quantum thermodynamic uncertainty relations (see Section S4 B).
We outline the standard collision model framework. Let N be the number of ancillae. The system S interacts with a sequence of ancillae A1, A2,  , AN, each identically prepared in the same state ρAn = ρA (Fig. S1(b)). For simplicity, we assume that the ancilla is a qubit with the Hamiltonian:  
HA = ϵ |1⟩ ⟨1| ,        (S68)
where ϵ > 0 is the energy gap between |0⟩ and |1⟩. Then, ρA is expressed as the Gibbs state:
e−βHA        e−βϵ        1
ρA = Tr[e−βHA ] = 1 + e−βϵ |1⟩ ⟨1| + 1 + e−βϵ |0⟩ ⟨0| .        (S69)      
Each system-ancilla interaction has a duration t and is implemented by the unitary USAn . After interacting with An, the state of S, ρ((n + 1)t), is given by
ρS((n + 1)t) = TrAn
________________
1USAn
________________
(ρS(nt) ⊗ ρAn
________________
†
SAn  
________________
i .        (S70)
We apply measurements to each ancillary in each step. Suppose that we apply the following Hermitian operator to each ancilla:
C = 1 |1⟩ ⟨1| + 0 |0⟩ ⟨0| .        (S71)
This acts as the qubit number operator that indicates whether the ancilla is excited. Let mn  0, 1 be the outcome when we measure An with C. Therefore, after interacting with N ancillae, we have a sequence of outputs m = [m1, m2,  , mN]. In effect, this procedure is equivalent to postponing all measurements and performing them all at once at the end (Fig. S1(b)). When we identify all the ancillae as the environment E, the environment Hamiltonian is

e−βHE = e−βHA1 ⊗ · · · ⊗ e−βHAN ,        (S74) which is the product of each ancilla Gibbs state. Moreover, the unitary applied to the environment is given by
U = USAN · · · USA2 USA1 .        (S75)
When we identify each measurement process as the joint measurement applied in the final state as shown in Fig. S1(b), the joint measurement operator is given by
CN ≡ L g(m) |m⟩ ⟨m| .        (S76)    
Here, g(m)        0 is an arbitrary non-negative function of m as long as g(0) = 0 is satisfied. This condition guarantees that the minimum eigenvalue of CN is 0. For example, suppose that g(m) is given by


N

n=1
In this case, CN is the number operator that counts the number of 1 in the ancillae.  
As noted above, the collision model forms the basis for the continuous measurement. Consequently, it is natural to compute the lower bound on the relative variance Var[g(m)]/E[g(m)]2. Using the fundamental precision limit [Eq. (10)], we can compute the lower bound on the relative variance Var[g(m)]/E[g(m)]2. The dimension of the environment is dE = 2N. Moreover, the energy gap is
∆ϵ = λmax(HE) − λmin(HE) = Nϵ.        (S78) Using these values, from Eq. (10), we obtain
Var[g(m)]
E [g(m)]2 ≥
________________
      2. eΦ(β,Nϵ,λmin(ρS ))
dS      
________________
−1
− 1        .        (S79)  
Equation (S79) is the precision limit in the collision model. Given that the dimension of the environment is exponential in N and that the energy gap ∆ϵ is proportional to N, the lower bound in Eq. (S79) is very loose.
In continuous measurements, the lower bound of the thermodynamic uncertainty relation is proportional to the inverse of the total time τ , which contrasts with the exponential dependence on N in Eq. (S79). This is because, whereas in continuous time the state change achievable in each step is limited, the discrete-time dynamics allows arbitrary unitary transformations. This feature is also observed in the thermodynamic uncertainty relation for classical Markov processes. Indeed, the lower bound of the discrete-time thermodynamic uncertainty relation is known to decrease exponentially with the number of steps [36, 37]. This is because discrete-time Markov processes permit state changes that are not allowed in continuous-time dynamics.

S7.        DIFFERENT INITIAL SETTINGS

S8.        NUMERICAL SIMULATION


We perform numerical simulations to verify Eq. (10). To do so, we randomly generate the density matrix of the system ρS and the environmental Hamiltonian HE, with the environment initially in the Gibbs state γE. We then apply a random unitary U and a random Hermitian operator G (with its minimum eigenvalue shifted to 0). By repeating this process multiple times, we obtain random realizations for both sides of Eq. (10): the left-hand side,
Var ′ [G]/E ′ [G]2, and the right-hand side, (d eΦ/d − 1)−1, which are plotted as points in Fig. S3(a). The dashed        
line in Fig. S3(a) represents the equality condition of the bound. As we can see, all the points lie above this line, indicating that Eq. (10) holds true in our simulation. Similarly, in Fig. S3(b), we verify Eq. (11) by plotting the left-hand side, Eρ′ [G], against the right-hand side, λmax[G] 1 − dSe−Φ/dE . Once again, the dashed line in Fig. S3(b) represents the equality case of Eq. (11), and our numerical results confirm the validity of Eq. (11).

Self-assembled clusters of mutually repelling particles in confinement
P. D. S. de Lima  ,1 R. De La Cour,2, 3 K. Gaff,2 J. M. de Arau´jo  ,1 S. J. Cox  ,4 M. S. Ferreira  ,2, 5 and S. Hutzler  2
1Departamento de F´ısica Te´orica e Experimental, Universidade Federal do Rio Grande do Norte, 59078-970 Natal-RN, Brazil
2School of Physics, Trinity College Dublin, Dublin 2, Ireland
3School of Physics, Technological University Dublin, Dublin 7, Ireland
4Department of Mathematics, Aberystwyth University, Aberystwyth, Ceredigion, SY23 3BZ, Wales, U.K.
5Centre for Research on Adaptive Nanostructures and Nanodevices (CRANN) & Advanced Materials and Bioengineering Research (AMBER) Centre, Trinity College Dublin, Dublin 2, Ireland (Dated: June 25, 2025)
Mutually repelling particles form spontaneously ordered clusters when forced into confinement. The clusters may adopt similar spatial arrangements even if the underlying particle interactions are contrastingly different. Here we demonstrate with both simulations and experiments that it is possible to induce particles of very different types to self-assemble into the same ordered geometric structure by simply regulating the ratio between the repulsion and confining forces. This is the case for both long- and short-ranged potentials. This property is initially explored in systems with two-dimensional (2D) circular symmetry and subsequently demonstrated to be valid throughout the transition to one-dimensional (1D) structures through continuous elliptical deformations of the confining field. We argue that this feature can be utilized to manipulate the spatial structure of confined particles, thereby paving the way for the design of clusters with specific functionalities.


1.   INTRODUCTION


Understanding the mechanisms that govern the formation and evolution of particle clusters is of fun-damental interest in scientific areas as diverse as sta-tistical physics [1, 2], condensed matter physics [3–5], and astrophysics, to name a few. The compos-ing particles may be magnetic [5–8] and/or ionic [9], thermally and/or optically active [10], and may range in size from the nanometer up to very large scales [11, 12]. The breadth of particle types and sizes of these clusters means that they may be con-trolled through different types of force fields, sug-gesting diverse applications [13–16]. The coexistence of two competing force fields acting on the particles is what drives their spontaneous clustering: one that traps them within a restricted area of space and a pairwise repulsion that prevents them from coalesc-ing into a high-density region.
Previous studies of such clusters (sometimes re-ferred to as ‘classical Wigner clusters’ [19]) have re-ported that particles may self-assemble into simi-lar spatial arrangements when their underlying in-teractions are not too different. Numerical results were obtained for particles placed in a harmonic (circular) confining potential and interacting via a Coulomb potential [20–22], but also for elliptic con-finement, with particles interacting via Coulomb [23] or a Yukawa potential [24, 25], or with a logarithmic force law [26]. In Ref. [25], a parameter could be var-
________________
ied to approximate arrangements of hard spheres in confinement; Ref. [17] is an online database of con-jectured optimal sphere packings in circular confine-ment, obtained computationally, for a large range of sphere numbers. Experimental studies include ar-rangements of magnetic particles [8, 27–30] or those interacting via a Coulomb potential [31, 32].
In this article, we argue that the conditions that interaction potentials must obey to generate clus-ters with the same equilibrium structure might be much less restrictive than previously thought. As a result, it is possible to identify systems that, at first glance, have very little in common with one an-other and yet display similar geometrical layouts. In fact, here we bring together examples of electrically charged particles, magnetic dipoles, hard spheres, and bubbles in a liquid foam, all under a common framework. We show that a simple model based on the repulsion-confinement ratio can describe cluster formation in widely diverse systems. This suggests a strong degree of universality of self-assembled par-ticle clusters in general, as seen in Table I where three of the aforementioned systems display similar geometrical arrangements, even though the nature and the range of the interaction between the parts are totally different.
Furthermore, given the expected sensitivity of the spatial arrangement of particles to system dimen-sionality, we focus on the evolution as particles ini-tially confined to a two-dimensional environment are

Published results regarding particles in elliptical confinement appear to be limited, motivating our ex-periments, depicted in Table I, and the simulations
________________
that follow. Saint Jean and Guthmann performed experiments with electrically charged stainless steel balls placed within a charged elliptic confinement [32]. The structures they found are consistent with our results, as will be discussed in Section VI. Sys-tematic computational results for particles confined within an ellipse also appear to be limited, due to
the much more demanding computation in determin-ing circle-ellipse overlaps. For the hard-sphere prob-lem, Birgin et al. describe a nonlinear optimization procedure which computes dense packings of discs within an ellipse [33]. (However, their main inter-est relates to algorithm efficiency.) Amore et al. [34] compute the variation in packing density with the shape of the ellipse for a limited range of numbers of particles.
The structure of the manuscript is as follows. The next section introduces the model that will guide us throughout the manuscript to describe the equilib-rium configurations of the particles in all the clus-ters. In addition, we demonstrate that under cer-tain conditions it is possible for a cluster to retain its equilibrium configuration even if the interaction between the composing particles is drastically mod-ified. Sections III and IV present a summary of ex-perimental and numerical results, respectively, ob-tained for a number of different systems. The par-ticle arrangements themselves are discussed in sec-tion V. Finally, Section VI addresses the potential impact of our findings and how they can be used in real applications.




   2. GUIDING MODEL


To illustrate our theoretical argument, we start by considering a confining region of size R and find-ing the exact equilibrium configuration of a cluster composed of particles interacting through the pair-wise central potential energy
u(r) = u0(r/R)−α ,        (1)
where r is the distance between particles and α is a dimensionless exponent. The constant u0 has di-mensions of energy, but its exact form depends on
________________
tance ρ as ρ = r/R we can then rewrite Eq. (1) in the dimensionless form


U (ρ) = u(r)/u0 = ρ−α .        (2)


For simplicity, it is convenient to have the Coulomb interaction as a reference, i.e. α = 1. Let us then consider a cluster composed of N charged particles in the presence of a circularly confining force field. We choose the latter to be generated by M charged particles whose charges are smaller than those of the cluster by a factor λ, where λ is a dimen-sionless positive constant. It is worth recalling that the cluster formation results from a competition be-tween confinement and repulsion, and the parameter λ plays a key role in regulating the balance between the two.
These M particles are uniformly distributed around the perimeter of a circle of radius R and fixed, whereas the N cluster-composing particles are allowed to move toward their equilibrium positions. Fig. 1(a) displays a schematic representation of this cluster with N = 5 (white dots) and M = 36 (not shown). Thanks to the circular symmetry combined with the central nature of the interactions, the clus-ter particles lie on a ring of radius ρ around the cen-tre. The value of λ determines the radius ρ, which gets larger (smaller) as λ increases (decreases). For the case illustrated in Fig. 1(a), finding ρ consists of carrying out an energy balance between the re-pulsive and the confining contributions to the total potential energy. More specifically, the total poten-tial energy of a particle is


Utot(ρ) = UC(ρ) + λUR(ρ) ,        (3)


where
the nature of the interaction. For example, it is pro-portional to the product of charges in the case of Coulomb interaction and to the product of magnetic moments in the case of interacting magnetic dipoles. The exponent α defines not only the type of particle

   1. Circular confinement


For particles contained within a circular confine-ment of radius R we introduce the dimensionless dis-
________________
Eq. (4) corresponds to the repulsion felt by one particle due to all the others in the cluster whereas Eq. (5) comes from the confining forces exerted by the M particles on the boundary. The indices j and k refer to the cluster and boundary parti-cles, respectively. Being in equilibrium means that
∂UC/∂ρ = −λ∂UR/∂ρ.
It is worth mentioning that, at this point, no re-strictions are being made on whether this equilib-rium configuration falls into a local or a global min-imum of the total potential energy Utot. Further-more, despite using α = 1 for generating Fig. 1(a), the experimental results appearing on the leftmost column of Tab. I are all very similar, even though none of them are mediated by the Coulomb interac-tion.
Regarding the generic forms of UR and UC writ-ten in terms of α, we impose no constraint on this exponent other than it having to be positive. It is then straightforward to see that the derivative of the repulsive term diverges in the limit that ρ → 0, de-caying monotonically to a finite value when ρ → 1. On the other hand, the derivative of the confining term vanishes at ρ = 0 and increases as ρ → 1. Therefore, there will always be a value of 0 ≤ ρ ≤ 1 for which ∂Utot/∂ρ = 0. This is illustrated in Fig. 1(b) , which plots −λ∂UR/∂ρ (solid blue line) and
∂UC/∂ρ (solid orange line) for the Coulomb case. Assuming λ = 1 in this instance, both curves cross at ρ = 0.49. A vertical dotted line indicates where the crossing occurs.
Let us now consider α ̸= 1. Since the repulsive contribution is modulated linearly by the parame-ter λ, it is always possible to find a suitable value for that quantity to keep the equilibrium position unchanged. In fact, the dashed lines of Fig. 1(b) correspond to α = 3, where λ = 2.2 has been ad-justed to maintain the solution at ρ = 0.49, i.e., such that both curves cross right where the vertical dotted line is placed. The inset of Fig. 1(b) displays the relationship between λ and α required to sustain the equilibrium position unchanged for three sepa-rate values of ρ, namely ρ = 0.2 (solid line), ρ = 0.5 (dashed line) and ρ = 0.8 (dot-dashed line). To sum-marise, we argue that a model with an adjustable repulsion-confinement ratio λ can be employed to describe the exact equilibrium configuration of par-ticle clusters in circular confinement, regardless of the underlying interaction. Strictly speaking, this argument works for a single shell but is sufficient to indicate the similarities in topology between differ-ent clusters.
The competition between repulsive and confining interactions is crucial for explaining particle clus-ter formation. As shown in Fig. 1, the repulsive potential must decay from the cluster center, while the confining potential must increase radially, both monotonically. This raises the question of what hap-pens when interactions become short-ranged (e.g., contact forces) instead of following a power law. Two examples in Tab.  I, hard spheres and soap
________________
bubbles in confinement, demonstrate short-ranged interactions. For these, the absence of forces when objects are not in contact requires a modified strat-egy to determine the appropriate value for the pa-rameter λ, different from the method in Fig. 1(b). We will discuss this next.
For the hard spheres, for example, it is possible to find close-packing solutions by following a simple procedure. We minimise the total energy defined in Eq. (3), with any value of α, say α = 1. We then proceed to find the value of λ by imposing as an additional criterion that the packing fraction (area of N = 5 circles divided by confinement area) must be maximum. Fig. 1(d) shows the solutions obtained for the case of N = 5. This corresponds to the packing fraction ϕ = 0.6845, which coincides with the exact value reported in Ref. [17].
A similar procedure is employed for the case of bubbles, the main difference being the criterion im-posed to specify the parameter λ. It is convenient to draw the particles as points (obtained through en-ergy minimisation with an arbitrary exponent α) to-gether with the Voronoi diagram, that is, a represen-tation in which the space is divided into polygonal-shaped regions that are closest to each of the parti-cles. Besides being a valuable visual aid, the Voronoi cells reflect the number of nearest neighbours a given particle has (through the number of edges), as well as the particle density (through the reciprocal of the polygonal area).
Our bubble experiments (Tab. I and Sec. III) were conducted with monodisperse bubbles. In our model, we can impose equal Voronoi cell areas Avor by finding a value of λ which minimises the area dis-persion σ2 = (1/N ) N (Avor − ⟨Avor⟩)2. This is shown in Fig. 1(c) for the case N = 5. It is impor-tant to stress that this simplified use of the guiding model serves in no way as a replacement to the stan-dard way of describing the geometry of liquid foams, described in Sec. IV A. The picture described here in terms of polygonal cells disregards the pressure dif-ferences between neighbouring bubbles, which lead to cell walls being arcs of circles and yet, remarkably, is capable of obtaining the same topologies, by which we mean the number of neighbours of each region, as the results depicted in Table I. The inset in Figure 1(c) shows that what at first glance may seem like a distinct topology in this highly-symmetrical particle arrangement, is actually identical to the one seen in Table I. Therefore, it is yet another piece of evidence supporting our claim that seemingly disparate phys-ical systems may display very similar arrangements.        


  



FIG. 1. (a) Colour plot indicating the total potential energy Utot given by Eq. (3) for N = 5 particles (seen as white dots) around a circular ring of radius ρ and by a total of M = 36 particles (not shown) homogeneously distributed at the circular boundary of unit radius. The value of λ = 1 was chosen arbitrarily and UR and UC are defined by Eqs.
(4) and (5) with α = 1. The white ring on which the N particles are located indicates the equipotential. The colour code is such that bright (dark) colours indicate relatively high (low) potential values. (b) Radial plots (in arbitrary units) of the derivatives −λ∂UR/∂ρ, shown in blue (on the left axis), and ∂UC/∂ρ, shown in orange (on the right axis). The solid (dashed) lines are for α = 1 and λ = 1 (α = 3 and λ = 2.2). The vertical dotted line indicates that the solid lines cross at exactly the same value of ρ = 0.49 as the dashed lines. The inset displays the relationship between λ and α required to have the N = 5 particles at equilibrium at values ρ = 0.2 (solid line), ρ = 0.5 (dashed line); ρ = 0.8 (dot-dashed line). (c) Voronoi structure obtained through a simple energy minimisation of the guiding model with N = 5 and λ selected through the criterion of minimizing the variance in the areas of the Voronoi cells. The central part, enlarged in the inset, is topologically equivalent to the corresponding soap bubble experiments in Table I. (d) Hard-sphere equilibrium configuration obtained through the same energy minimisation procedure, but this time by adjusting λ to maximise the volume fraction of the cluster.


   2. Elliptic confinement


A similar argument to the one just presented ap-plies when the symmetry of the confining field is not circular but elliptic.  A single parameter λ is not
________________
sufficient to determine the required equilibrium con-figuration. We use, in addition, the eccentricity ϵ of the ellipse. Then, if the nature of the interaction changes, say from α = 1 (Coulomb) to α = 3 (mag-netic dipolar), it is possible to maintain the same


  





FIG. 2. (a) Example of identical equilibrium configu-ration for N = 5 particles under different interaction potentials. The orange stars correspond to the Coulomb case (α = 1, λ = 1.3, ϵ = 0.91) and the blue circles to the magnetic case (α = 3, λ = 1, ϵ = 0.87), both with M = 36 and ellipses in the same colour. (b) This plot indicates the relationship between the eccentricity of the confining ellipse and the exponent α required to main-tain a given equilibrium configuration. The four points highlighted at α = 3 are the elliptical eccentricity values seen in Table I, namely ϵ = 0.44 (blue triangle), 0.66 (orange triangle), 0.87 (green triangle) and 0.95 (red triangle), for the case of N = 5 floating magnets. (c) This plot indicates how the parameter λ must change with α to maintain the same four equilibrium configura-tions highlighted in panel (b).
________________
equilibrium configuration of the particles if the ec-centricity of the confining ellipse is also changed. This is shown in Fig. 2(a), where two sets of N = 5 particles interacting via a different potential are con-fined within ellipses of different eccentricities. When superposed, the equilibrium positions of the two sets are the same, bar small numerical deviations. In other words, similarly to the circular case seen in Fig. 1, it is possible to identify the same equilibrium configurations of particle clusters that are governed by distinct interactions. In the same spirit, the thick solid line in Fig. 2(b) indicates what eccentricity val-ues ϵ one would need to produce the same equilib-rium configuration as in panel (a) for values of α other than 1 and 3. In addition, three other curves are shown reflecting the precise equilibrium config-urations found for the floating magnets in Table I. Finally, the four configurations seen in panel (b) will remain the same if the parameter λ changes with α as shown in panel (c). This feature once again high-lights a high degree of universality in the formation of self-assembled clusters, suggesting that these sim-ilarities are likely to occur even beyond the circular and elliptical confinements considered here.
In summary, the guiding model encapsulates mu-tually repelling particles under the action of confin-ing forces. Once the confinement-repulsion ratio is adjusted together with the eccentricity, it is possible to find the same equilibrium configurations regard-less of the underlying interaction potentials. In the next section we describe how we were able to exper-imentally generate similar equilibrium structures in clusters governed by very different physical interac-tions (using hard spheres, magnetic particles, and bubbles; for Coulomb interactions see [31, 32]), all of which under the action of circular and elliptical confining fields.








   3. EXPERIMENTAL REALISATIONS OF PACKINGS IN ELLIPSOIDAL
CONFINEMENT


In both the experiments described in this sec-tion and the simulations of section IV, we restrict ourselves to elliptical confinements of five different values of eccentricity, ϵ ∈ {0, 0.44, 0.66, 0.87, 0.95}, where ϵ = 1 − (b/a)2, with a and b denoting re-spectively the lengths of the semi-major and semi-minor ellipse axes. Experimental results are de-scribed for N = 5 and N = 10 particles.    
   1. Experiments with hard spheres


Experimenting with dense packings of hard spheres is probably the simplest way to illustrate the role that confinement plays in the formation of or-dered arrangements. The structures encountered for given values of eccentricity ϵ, and number of spheres N , will also be identified in our experiments with floating magnets and bubbles.
Since we are only considering a monolayer of spheres, the packings are equivalent to the prob-lem of the densest two-dimensional packings of non-overlapping hard disks in confinement. In this con-text, the problem may be stated as follows. Given an ellipse of area A and eccentricity ϵ, what is the area Ac, of identical circles that maximise the pack-ing fraction ϕ = NAc/A, for a given number N of circles.
For the case of a circular confinement, i.e. ϵ = 0, there is an online database [17] of conjectured op-timal packings, obtained computationally, for val-ues of N up to 2604. However, systematic compu-tational results for confining ellipses appear to be limited, due to the much more demanding compu-tation in determining circle-ellipse overlaps. Birgin et al. describe a nonlinear optimization procedure which computes dense packings of more than 300 circles within an ellipse [33]. However, their main interest relates to algorithm efficiency. Amore et al. compute the variation in packing density with the length of the semi-major axis for three values of N (37, 61, 91) [34]; some further simulation results are
displayed by [18].
Here we describe an illustrative experimental ap-proach to obtain dense sphere packings in confine-ment. It involves the use of sets of metal spheres (with a different sphere diameter in each set) and 3D-printed elliptical frames of different areas for the five different values of eccentricity, as stated above. We validate our results by comparing with conjectured optimizers for N = 5, ϵ = 0.867 and N = 10, ϵ = 0.6539 [18].
To obtain a dense sphere packing for a given el-liptical frame, we proceed as follows. The frame is placed on a horizontal surface, and we manually dis-pose spheres of a given size into it. This process does generally not result in a dense packing, i.e. there comes a point when there is enough room available for the spheres to rattle, but not enough room for the addition of a further sphere, without losing contact with the horizontal surface. We thus start the pro-cess again, but now with slightly larger, or smaller spheres. By trial and error we minimise the amount of free space to arrive at a (near) dense configura-
________________
tion.
Table I shows photographs of the arrangements that we consider sufficiently space-filling to demon-strate a dense packing. For the circular confinement, ϵ = 0 (for which we have simulated data to com-pare with [17]), we reproduce the conjectured dens-est arrangements for both N = 5 and N = 10. Our experimentally obtained values for the packing frac-tions are ϕ5 = 0.68 ± 0.01 and ϕ10 = 0.69 ± 0.01; this is consistent with the published results ϕ5 = 5/(1 + 1/ sin(π/5))2 ≃ 0.685 and ϕ10 ≃ 0.688 [17]. Also our results for N = 5, ϵ = 0.87, ϕ = 0.68 ± 0.01 and N = 10, ϵ = 0.66, ϕ = 0.77 ± 0.01 are consis-tent with those of simulations (ϕ5 = 0.6869, ϕ10 = 0.7797) [18].




   2. Experiments with floating magnets


Experiments with mutually repelling magnets, floating on water, and subject to an external mag-netic confining field, are generally traced back to the 19th century physicist Alfred M. Mayer [27, 28]. His main interest, and that of many others who followed,
e.g. [8, 29, 30, 35], was the identification of equilib-rium structures for a circularly symmetric confining field. Here we report experiments in which the con-fining field has elliptical symmetry, with the same values of eccentricity as given above.
Our experiments were performed using floaters made from 3D-printed hollow cylinders, open at the top (diameter 22.4 mm, height 25 mm), as illus-trated in Fig. 3(a). A metallic rod (length 23 mm, diameter 4.5 mm) was led through a hole at the bot-tom, and two small neodymium disc magnets (di-ameter 5 mm, each of thickness 1.4 mm) (purchased from [36]) were attached to it at the bottom end. When placed into a pool of water, the magnet-fitted cylinders float, with the magnets being submerged in the water.
We also 3D-printed four elliptical frames, with the same cross-sectional area of π cm2 but different ec-centricities. Bar magnets (‘toy magnetic building sticks’) were attached along the inner perimeter of each frame, in opposite orientation to that of the floating magnets, and thus providing a confining magnetic field. The same number of magnets, 42, was used for all of the frames; since the length of the perimeter of an ellipse increases with eccentric-ity, ϵ, this results in a number density of magnets which decreases with ϵ.
The magnet-fitted elliptical frame rests on the bottom of a pool of water, and its rim projects above the water level. Magnetic floaters were then placed

   3. Experiments with monolayers of bubbles


Arrays of soap bubbles floating on a liquid (intro-duced by Bragg and Nye [37] to model crystal struc-ture) serve as another simple quasi-2D experimental system to demonstrate the formation of structures with a now familiar topology when in confinement. Similar to the hard spheres of Section III A, bub-bles experience repelling forces only when they are in contact with each other or with the confining frame. In the following, we describe experiments per-formed with monolayers of equal-volume bubbles, confined within 3D-printed elliptical frames of equal area but different eccentricity. The bubbles were produced by blowing air at constant pressure through a nozzle into a pool of aqueous surfactant solution; see Fig. 3(b). (We chose the commercial detergent “Fairy Liquid” as it is known to produce stable foams.) The elliptical frame is placed at the surface of the liquid pool, so that the rising bub-bles aggregate within it, with their vertical exten-sion slightly exceeding the height of the frame rim.
________________
Once the frame is filled with a monolayer of bub-bles, a glass plate is used to cover it. Since the plate is also contacting the bubbles, this leads to a two-dimensional confined covered Bragg raft, as was pioneered in the experiments by [38]). This raft is viewed from top, resulting in an image of a two-dimensional cellular structure.




The number of bubbles, N , that can be placed within an ellipse of given area depends on the size of the monodisperse bubbles. However, the quasi-2D character of the experiment, i.e. the finite vertical dimension of the raft, allows for the accommodation of a range of bubble sizes for a given value of N .




We have gathered data for N = 5 and 10, for a total of five different elliptical frames (eccentricity ϵ = 0, 0.44, 0.66, 0.87, 0.95) and fixed frame cross-section A = π cm2. The equivalent sphere diameters of the bubbles in use were 3.0mm (N = 5), 2.8mm (N = 10). The height of the frame rim above the waterline was about 2.0mm.




For each pair of numbers of bubbles N and ec-centricity ϵ, we conducted at least 10 experimental runs. This enabled the identification of several al-ternative bubble configurations. Table I shows the subset of structures which correspond to configura-tions of minimal energy, as identified in our com-puter simulations of 2D foams, described in Section IV A. Further configurations are shown in Appendix A, Table II.
(a)
  

(b)

The topology of the least-perimeter cluster is the same for 0 ≤ ϵ < 0.93, above which there are two further arrangements of the bubbles. (b) N = 10. The topology of the least-perimeter cluster is the same for 0 ≤ ϵ < 0.84, above which there are several different ar-rangements of the bubbles, not all shown.




   4. COMPUTER SIMULATIONS OF CLUSTERS IN ELLIPTICAL CONFINEMENT


   1. Simulations of two-dimensional foams
________________
The energy of a 2D foam is proportional to the total length of the soap films (the perimeter); we find local minima of the perimeter to high accuracy. We ex-amine many local minima from which we conjecture the global minimum for each N and ϵ.
The method is based on the one described in [42]: for each N and ϵ, we find an approximation to the equilibrium position of N particles confined in an el-lipse and interacting via a Coulomb interaction po-tential (described in more detail in Section IV B). These then act as seed points for a Voronoi con-struction, as described above. The Voronoi regions are then imported into Surface Evolver and treated as bubbles (with curved edges), and local minimum of the total perimeter is found by gradient descent. This is followed by a Metropolis-like “shuffling” procedure, in which we delete an edge, either chosen at random or the edge with the shortest length, to see if the foam perimeter is decreased – if it is, the new foam structure is kept, else it is rejected. This process results in a conjectured minimal foam for each (N, ϵ). Finally, for each of these foams, we vary ϵ over a small range (roughly ±0.2) as a check that the foam structure of lowest perimeter has been found also at nearby eccentricities. These results are
in Fig. 4.




   2. Simulations of floating magnets


To predict the arrangement of floating magnets, we treat them as classical particles that interact via a pair-wise potential and are subject to a confining potential, which is a particular case of the guiding model presented in Sec. II with α = 3. However, we stress that our method would be the same for the Coulomb case (α = 1) [20–22, 25].
In this regard, we obtain equilibrium configura-tions of N interacting magnetic particles possessing a magnetic moment m, which are geometrically con-fined in a two-dimensional domain by M fixed mag-nets with magnet moment mext on the boundary of an ellipse. Assuming that all magnet moments involved are aligned with the z-direction, the total energy can be written as

   5. COMPARISON OF THE DIFFERENT SYSTEMS


In Sect. III we have presented experimental data for three different systems: hard spheres, floating magnets, and bubbles. All consist of a discrete set of interacting particles, confined by an external po-tential. These ingredients are sufficient to result in

Hard sphere interactions are discontinuous and only act upon contact; they are usually modelled using a pairwise additive Hertzian potential [47]. Bubbles also interact via contact forces. However,
where Utot = 4πa3u/(µ0mmext) with λ = mext/m. Moreover, we have defined the dimensionless coordi-nates ηi = xi/a and ξi = yi/b which are constraints to η2 + ξ2 ≤ 1.

in contrast to hard spheres, the change in shape which they undergo upon contact results in non-local forces, featuring a logarithmic term [48]. The float-ing magnets, on the other hand, are not governed by
i        i
contact forces, but by long-range dipole interaction.
We use the generalised simulated annealing (GSA) method [43–46] to efficiently minimize Eq. (7) for the 2N equilibrium positions (ηi, ξi). We proceed similarly as in Ref. [8] and calibrate λ for a partic-ular experiment, namely N = 5 and ϵ = 0, in which we obtain λ = 1.3. The equilibrium configurations obtained in this case are shown in Fig. 5, consider-ing the N = 5 and N = 10 cases. Remarkably, we can reproduce the experimental observations very well regardless of the eccentricity using the same calibrated λ. It is expected, since this parameter depends solely on the balance between interparticle repulsion and confinement.
________________
We studied arrangements of N = 5 and N = 10 particles of the above kinds, placed in an elliptic confining potential. The arrangements reveal topo-logically similar sequences of structures for the five different values of eccentricity, ϵ, that we probed. In the following we will summarise these findings.
For N = 5 and ϵ = 0 the centers of the particles (hard spheres/floating magnets/bubbles) can be re-garded as the vertices of a (regular) pentagon (as in Fig. 1). The arrangement becomes increasingly distorted for larger values of ϵ, leading to the loss of contacts of nearest neighbours. In the limit ϵ → 1 the only possible structure is that of a straight line
of particles (see Table I for floating magnets, and Table II for bubbles); in the case of hard spheres or floating magnets this straight line formed by parti-cle centers is preceded by a zig-zag arrangement (or ‘buckled’ line), which straightens out with increasing ϵ.
For bubbles the zig-zag structure is replaced by an arrangement with three bubble centers situated along the major axis, and two on a line perpendic-ular to it, but not coinciding with the minor axis (see Table I, ϵ = 0.95). The same arrangement can also be obtained for our floating magnets (Table II, ϵ = 0.87), but in this case it corresponds to a higher energy configuration than the zig-zag struc-ture. The arrangement was also observed by Saint Jean and Guthmann [32] in their experiments with steel spheres under the influence of Coulomb forces, for similar values of ϵ. Apolonario et al. encountered it in their simulations of particles interacting via a repulsive logarithmic potential [26].
An alternative arrangement for ϵ = 0 is to have one particle in the centre, surrounded by four parti-cles in contact1 with the frame (or close to it, in the case of the floating magnets). This is shown in Ap-pendix A, Table II, for bubbles, floating magnets, and hard spheres. The corresponding 2D packing fraction of circles, ϕ = 5/9, is lower than that for the case when all circles are in contact with the frame, with ϕ5 = 0.685 [17] (see section III A). Also for 2D foams, this arrangement is not optimal; our simula-tions show that it is of higher energy than having all bubbles in contact with the frame. In our foam experiments we were able to produce the higher en-ergy structure for values of ϵ =0, 0.44, and 0.66 (see Table II). In the case of floating magnets, the con-figuration was only stable at ϵ = 0. We associate the larger stability range found for foams with the fact that they constitute a ‘jammed system’, requiring larger forces for a possible restructuring.
For N = 10, a larger number of possible arrange-ments exist, see Tables I and II; also here a simi-larity in the sequence of the ‘optimal’ structures for different values of ϵ is apparent. Let us first turn to our sphere packings, which we will again interpret as a packing of circles. The densest packing of ten circles within a circle contains two circles in the cen-tre, surrounded by eight circles in contact with the frame [17]. Each of the two central circles is in con-tact with three circles. Two of the perimeter circles contact three circles each, the remaining six have
  



1 We infer contacts between spheres from the images, rather than detecting them directly.
________________
two contacts each. In our experiments with hard spheres this configuration is found also for ϵ = 0.44 and 0.66.
The foam structure of minimal energy resembles the hard sphere arrangement in the following sense. It too consist consists of two particles (bubbles) in the centre, which are surrounded by eight bubbles which contact the frame. Also here two types of boundary particles can be identified. There are 6 bubbles contacting three bubbles each, and 2 bub-bles contacting four bubbles each. Simulations show that this arrangement is that of lowest energy for ϵ < 0.84, in our experiments we found it for ϵ = 0, 0.44, and 0.66.
Also the arrangement of floating magnets (in both experiments and simulations) features two central and eight boundary magnets for ϵ = 0, 0.44, and
0.66. In experiments using charged spheres the same arrangement was reported for ϵ = 0.71 [32].
At ϵ = 0.87 the structure differs from that at lower ϵ in all three systems. The hard sphere structure still features two spheres that are not in contact with the frame, but each has gained an additional con-tact. The contact distribution of the eight bound-ary spheres is unchanged (6 × 2 contacts, and 2 × 3 contacts). Also the foam has still two bubbles in the centre, but each lost a side and now only con-tacts five boundary bubbles. Of these there are still two types, six with four bubble contacts, and two with only two contacts. The magnet configuration is probably best described as four staggered rows of two magnets, each aligned perpendicular to the ma-jor axis, and one magnet each close to the endpoints of the major axis. Two of the magnets located to-wards the centre of the ellipse are furthest away from the frame and in this sense might be defined as the two non-boundary particles that are seen in both foams and hard sphere arrangements.
At ϵ = 0.95 all bubbles and all hard spheres are in contact with the frame, and there are also no more boundary magnets, in the sense defined above. The configurations in the three different systems all have 180◦ rotational symmetry.
This type of arrangement is reminiscent of a line of hard spheres, placed in a transverse potential, which has buckled under compression [49, 50]. It is tempt-ing to pursue this analogy, in order to explore what structural changes this would imply for this ‘zipper structure’ [50] at an even higher value of ϵ, corre-sponding to a weaker compression along the major axis. As particle contacts gradually disappear (the zipper ‘unzips’), starting from the endpoints of the major axis (where there is no more room for pairs of particles), one arrives at a constellation, called the
‘doublet’ [50], in which all particles are aligned, ex-cept one pair close to the centre. A corresponding foam configuration is that for N = 5 at ϵ = 0.95 in Table I, a corresponding magnet configuration is shown in Table II for N = 5 and ϵ = 0.87. The suggested analogy to the buckling experiments by [49, 50] also suggests the possibility of a ‘modulated zig-zag structure’ [51] in which the deviation of par-ticle centres from the major-axis varies along the axis, with a maximum near the centre, before at even higher values of ϵ (i.e. lower compression) a straight line is formed. We indeed found such a modulated zig-zag formation in some preliminary ex-periments with a larger number of magnetic floaters. This could open up avenues for a theoretical analysis similar to those of Ref. [51] in terms of a continuum model for structure formation.




   6. DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION


Based on the guiding model of Section II we have seen that mutually repelling particles in the pres-ence of confining forces self-assemble into clusters with topologies that are primarily determined by the confinement-repulsion ratio. More specifically, in the case of circularly symmetric confinements, we have seen that it is possible to generate clus-ters with identical topologies even though the na-ture and range of the underlying interactions may be totally different. In addition, the same is possible in the case of elliptically symmetric confining forces, the main difference being that the eccentricity of the confining ellipse may be adjusted, together with the ratio of confining and repelling forces, in order to establish the exact cluster geometry. These findings suggest that it is possible to engineer cluster geome-tries whose topologies may display certain desirable functionalities [52, 53]. Bearing in mind that clus-ters of magnetic particles are crucial in many fields, including targeted drug delivery [54], microwave ab-sorption [55], quantum computing [56], molecular magnets and magneto-optical technologies [57–59], being able to engineer the precise topology of such structures will only widen the range of applications for these clusters.
In the experiments that we presented, particles were placed into an elliptical frame of given eccen-tricity, ϵ, and we recorded the resulting structure. In an alternative procedure one could vary ϵ con-tinuously, keeping the area of the ellipse constant,
________________
with the particles remaining confined. (This would not be possible for the hard sphere packings as their packing fraction varies with ϵ.) At some critical val-ues of eccentricity, rearrangements would occur. We would also expect hysteresis, i.e. different critical values, and even structures, depending on whether ϵ is increased or decreased. Experiments of this form might be difficult to devise, but simulations are pos-sible. Indeed, the foam simulations shown in Fig. 4 were already performed by varying ϵ in small incre-ments, for a fixed configuration. However, since we were only interested in global energy minima, we did not determine the ranges of stability for the different structures.
In all of the above we have only considered the (static) equilibrium configurations. The experi-ments using the floating magnets offer the oppor-tunity to also study the dynamics. In particular some of the metastable configurations are associated with an often minute-long “dance” of the floaters before they settle into equilibrium. Once this is achieved, individual floaters can be manually dis-placed to probe the impact this shows on the other floaters. The rate of relaxation towards the old or a new equilibrium configuration indicates the steep-ness of the energy gradient. In our experiments with floating magnets we observed ‘marginally’ sta-ble structures that took much longer than others to reach equilibrium. In addition, moving particular magnets can have a more significant impact on the positions of the other magnets, leading to the pos-sibility to identify particles which have some free-dom of movement and others that are “locked” into position. This may be related to floppy modes in condensed matter.

Reasoning
Providing tools to translate stochastic to telegraphic measures
I'm outlining key mathematical tools—like Herglotz and Stieltjes transforms, DtN maps, spectral measures, and stochastic homogenization—to represent stochastic boundary measures as positive-real telegraphic operators. The reply will propose building an effective deterministic DtN via averaging and spectral analysis, separating variance via martingale decompositions, and leveraging harmonic analysis to bridge random boundary operators to telegrapher's equations, thus mapping stochastic to telegraphic measures in a structured pipeline.
Here’s a compact “dictionary” you can use to move between a stochastic boundary measure (e.g., GMC) and a telegraphic/positive-real (PR) boundary operator in the TEP sense. The key is that any finite Borel measure $\nu$ on the boundary can be encoded by an analytic, PR/Herglotz function via its Stieltjes/Fourier transform; PR functions are exactly what TEP manipulates.

1) Represent stochastic measures as PR/Herglotz objects
- Herglotz–Stieltjes transform: For a finite positive measure $\nu$ on $\mathbb{R}$, $$m(z) = \int_{\mathbb{R}} \frac{1}{t - z}\, \nu(dt), \quad \Im z > 0,$$ defines a Herglotz function with $\Im m(z) \ge 0$. The boundary value $\Im m(\omega + i0)$ recovers the density if it exists. In TEP, the boundary admittance $Y(i\omega)$ is PR (i.e., a Herglotz function up to factors of $i$). Thus a random measure induces a random PR function $Y(i\omega)$.
- Carathéodory / Schur transforms (on the unit circle): For $\nu$ on $\mathbb{T}$, $$F(z) = \int_{\mathbb{T}} \frac{e^{i\theta}+z}{e^{i\theta}-z}\, \nu(d\theta), \quad |z|<1,$$ is Carathéodory (positive real part). This is the circular analogue of the Herglotz transform; $F$ is PR and its boundary values encode $\nu$.
- Kramers–Kronig / Hilbert transform: If $Y(i\omega) = G(\omega) + i B(\omega)$ is PR, then $$B(\omega) = \mathcal{H}G, \quad G(\omega) = -\mathcal{H}B,$$ with $\mathcal{H}$ the Hilbert transform. This makes explicit the passivity/causality constraint for any realization of the random measure.

2) From stochastic to telegraphic (annealed/coarse-grained) operators
- Annealed PR operator: Define the effective admittance as the expectation of the random Herglotz transform, $$\bar{Y}(i\omega) := \mathbb{E}\big[ Y_{\nu}(i\omega) \big],$$ which is still PR. This is the telegraphic/coarse-grained boundary law. Variance and higher cumulants quantify stochastic departures.
- Homogenization / self-averaging: For log-correlated fields (GMC), at scales larger than a correlation length $\ell$, $Y(i\omega)$ self-averages. You can split $$Y(i\omega) = \bar{Y}(i\omega) + \delta Y(i\omega),$$ with $\bar{Y}$ telegraphic, $\delta Y$ fluctuating. The Rajchman property corresponds to $\delta Y$ having vanishing high-frequency Fourier coefficients.
- Doob–Meyer / martingale decomposition: For multiplicative chaos measures, the regularized boundary laws are martingales in the cutoff scale. The predictable (drift) part gives the telegraphic component; the martingale part is the stochastic correction.

3) Translating spectral regularity (Rajchman) to boundary regularity
- Rajchman $\Leftrightarrow$ PR decay: If $\nu$ is Rajchman, then $Y(i\omega)$ decays in the sense that its boundary values have vanishing Fourier coefficients. In TEP language: the random admittance is “high-frequency transparent”.
- Capacity/energy criteria: The $s$-energy $$\iint \frac{\nu(dx)\,\nu(dy)}{|x-y|^{s}} < \infty$$ controls the $\ell^{2}$ decay of Fourier coefficients: $$\sum_{n} |n|^{s-1} |\hat{\nu}(n)|^{2} < \infty.$$ This is exactly a bound on the $L^{2}$ norm of $Y(i\omega)$ weighted by $|\omega|^{s-1}$, hence a boundary-regularity criterion for the PR operator.

4) Practical pipeline to build a TEP translation for random measures
1. Encode: Map $\nu$ (real line or circle) to a PR/Herglotz function via Stieltjes or Carathéodory transform.
2. Anneal: Take $\mathbb{E}[\,\cdot\,]$ to get a telegraphic $\bar{Y}$; keep $\delta Y$ for fluctuations.
3. Enforce passivity: Use Kramers–Kronig to tie real/imag parts of $\bar{Y}$ (and each realization $Y$).
4. Quantify decay: Use capacity/energy exponents $s$ to bound Fourier decay and thus boundary smoothness of $\bar{Y}$; compare to GMC exponents $s_*(\gamma)$.
5. Invert if needed: To go back from $\bar{Y}$ to an effective measure, invert the Herglotz/Carathéodory transform (Stieltjes inversion) to get the “telegraphic” density.

5) Contextual tools to bridge to “telegrapher” PDEs
- Telegrapher’s equation $\leftrightarrow$ PR admittance: A 1D telegrapher has impedance $$Z(i\omega) = R + i \omega L + \frac{1}{i\omega C} + \dots,$$ which is PR. Random boundary measures induce random $Z$; averaging gives a telegraphic effective line. The above transforms let you swap between the random measure view and the PDE boundary-value view.
- SPDE / Feynman–Kac: For stochastic boundary weights, you can model propagation with random boundary conditions; Feynman–Kac gives expectations of solutions as integrals against the random measure, again leading to a PR averaged operator.

If you like, I can draft a short note showing the explicit mapping for the ellipse-packing data: given sampled point sets, build an empirical measure $\hat{\nu}$ on the boundary, take its Carathéodory transform to get $\hat{Y}(z)$, fit a smooth telegraphic $\bar{Y}$ (PR) to it, and then compare the inferred $\bar{Y}$ across different microscopic interactions (hard/soft/dipolar) to demonstrate the universality in TEP terms.

Reasoning
Clarifying classical nature of transforms
These transforms are standard tools from classical harmonic analysis, spectral theory, and complex function theory; the novelty lies in their application combining random measures with telegrapher-type boundary operators, not in the transforms themselves. I'll emphasize they encode positive measures as analytic functions via known inversion formulas and their connection to spectral measures, while cautioning about inherent limitations and the persistence of analytic (not arithmetic) structure.
What you are doing there is not a new “mysterious transform”, it is a very well–trodden dictionary between measures and analytic functions:
- The Herglotz/Carathéodory–Schur transforms and their cousins in the Stieltjes/Cauchy family encode any finite positive measure as a holomorphic function with positive real part. The inversion formulas and moment expansions go back to Nevanlinna and are the bread‑and‑butter of spectral theory.
- In electrical engineering, the same class of functions are exactly the positive–real impedances/admittances that solve the telegrapher’s equation; in probability and operator theory they are the spectral measures of self–adjoint/unitary operators; in random matrix and free probability they appear as Cauchy transforms of empirical laws.
- For random measures, you get a random Herglotz/PR function. Taking expectations or coarse–graining gives you a deterministic PR function (the “telegraphic” effective law), with fluctuations controlled by the variance of the transform. You don’t need to know individual eigenvalues: the entire distribution is encoded in the transform, and the transform is recoverable from moments or correlation structure.
So the pieces are 100 % classical. The novelty, if any, is in how you package them for your application: using this dictionary to translate a GMC‑type random boundary weight into a TEP boundary operator, or to argue about high‑frequency transparency (Rajchman) in the language of passivity. It won’t conjure arithmetic structure out of nothing; you are still working in the analytic/Herglotz class. But it does give you a rigorous way to pass between “random/discrete/unbounded” inputs and “PR/telegraph” outputs without explicit spectral data, provided you control basic growth/positivity conditions.

Reasoning
Explaining transform dependence on sampling and geometry
The transformed field encodes either the underlying hyperbolic measure if sampling is uniform or the empirical distribution with scrambling noise; hyperbolic geometry shapes growth and scaling, while random sampling adds fluctuations that vanish asymptotically, with the protocol output reflecting both factors through measure pushforward and the Herglotz transform.
If you unpack the protocol, the only thing it ever “sees” is the measure you feed it. In your thought experiment there are two moving parts:
- The smooth hyperbolic metric and whatever canonical volume/area form it gives you. If you push that measure to a one‑dimensional boundary (e.g. via a Poisson kernel on the disc) and look at its Carathéodory/Herglotz transform, you’ll get a very rigid, deterministic PR function dictated by the geometry: constant curvature, invariance under the isometry group, growth at the boundary, etc.
- The way you “scramble” or subsample it. A finite random sample is just an empirical measure. Its transform is a random PR function that fluctuates around the transform of the underlying measure. As the sample size grows, a law of large numbers kicks in and you recover the underlying transform; with heavy biasing or thinning you can change the limiting law.
If you “parabolically unbind” the measure or rescale by a (G)–factor, that is simply defining a different measure. The mapped PR field will faithfully reflect both the underlying geometry through whatever base measure you started from, and the sampling/weighting scheme through noise and bias. There’s nothing in the transform that can separate “geometry” from “sampling” unless you control one of them: deterministic inputs give deterministic outputs; random inputs give random outputs with deterministic limits if the sampling is consistent.

Reasoning
Outlining Carathéodory/Herglotz Transform Recipe
I'm drafting a concise outline for applying the Carathéodory/Herglotz transform to packing data, focusing on empirical measure construction, formula for the transform $F(z)$, and fitting a positive-real function via rational approximation or boundary least squares. The output will include key formulas with inline math notation and a note on the target result.
Here’s a concrete outline and the key steps to turn your packing data into a “telegraphic” positive‑real (PR) boundary operator, and to show universality across interactions.

Outline for the short note
1. Setup and data
	o Describe the configurations (hard spheres, magnets, bubbles) in ellipses with eccentricity $\epsilon$.
	o Represent each configuration as an empirical measure $\hat{\nu}$ on the boundary (e.g. normalized sum of Diracs at boundary contact points, or at projected particle centers).
2. Measure $\to$ PR transform
	o On the unit circle: Carathéodory transform $$F(z) = \int_{0}^{2\pi} \frac{e^{i\theta}+z}{e^{i\theta}-z}\,\hat{\nu}(d\theta), \quad |z|<1.$$ $F$ has $\Re F>0$; boundary values $F(e^{i\phi})$ encode $\hat{\nu}$.
	o On the real line: Herglotz–Stieltjes transform $$m(z) = \int_{\mathbb{R}} \frac{1}{t - z}\,\hat{\nu}(dt), \quad \Im z>0,$$ with $\Im m\ge 0$. Use if you parametrize along an interval.
3. PR fit / telegraphic operator
	o Sample $F$ (or $m$) on a grid $z=r e^{i\phi}$ with $r\uparrow 1$.
	o Fit a low‑order PR function $\bar{F}$ (e.g. rational with positive real part) to the sampled values via least squares, enforcing $\Re \bar{F}\ge 0$ and Hilbert (Kramers–Kronig) consistency.
	o Define the “telegraphic” admittance $\bar{Y}(i\omega)$ as the boundary values of $\bar{F}$.
4. Quantify fluctuations and universality
	o Compute residuals $\delta F = F - \bar{F}$; summarize their magnitude across datasets.
	o Show that $\bar{F}$ (and thus $\bar{Y}$) is essentially invariant across hard/dipolar/bubble cases at fixed $(N,\epsilon)$, demonstrating universality.
5. Interpretation
	o $\bar{Y}$ is the coarse‑grained boundary law; small $\delta F$ means microscopic interaction details wash out.
	o Discuss how decay/regularity of $\hat{\nu}$ (e.g. vanishing Fourier coefficients) manifests as high‑frequency transparency of $\bar{Y}$.
6. Next steps
	o Suggest dynamic tests (relaxation/admittance vs. $\epsilon$) or extensions to stochastic measures (GMC) using the same transforms.

Recipe in more detail
- Empirical measure: For $K$ sample points ${\theta_k}$ on the boundary, $$\hat{\nu} = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{k=1}^K \delta_{\theta_k}.$$
- Carathéodory transform (circle): $$F(z) = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{k=1}^K \frac{e^{i\theta_k}+z}{e^{i\theta_k}-z}.$$
- Herglotz transform (interval $I$): $$m(z) = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{k=1}^K \frac{1}{t_k - z}, \quad t_k\in I.$$
- Boundary sampling: Evaluate $F(r e^{i\phi_j})$ for $\phi_j$ on a uniform grid and $r$ close to $1$.
- PR fitting: Choose an ansatz $\bar{F}(z)=\sum_{n=0}^M a_n z^n$ or a rational form with constraints to ensure $\Re \bar{F}>0$. Solve $$\min_{a_n}\sum_j \left|F(r e^{i\phi_j}) - \bar{F}(r e^{i\phi_j})\right|^2$$ subject to PR constraints (e.g. positive real part at the sample points).
- Recover admittance: On the boundary $z=e^{i\phi}$, set $\bar{Y}(i\omega)\equiv \bar{F}(e^{i\phi})$ with $\omega\leftrightarrow \phi$; real and imaginary parts are linked by the Hilbert transform.
- Universality check: Repeat for each dataset; compare the fitted $\bar{F}$ across interaction types. Plot residual norms to show small differences.
This will give you a clear, reproducible bridge from discrete/random boundary measures to smooth telegraphic PR operators, using only standard transforms. Once you’ve run the fits and have a couple of plots, the note should be in a good shape to circulate.
